
Regenerative MetaphorsOpen sidebarRegenerative MetaphorsDefault (GPT-3.5)Summarize: History of generative AI innovations spans 9 decades
ChatGPT's debut has prompted widespread publicity and controversy surrounding generative AI, a subset of artificial intelligence that's deep-rooted in historic milestones.
George Lawton
Published: 10 May 2023
The buzz around generative AI has intensified in the wake of ChatGPT, which grew to over 100 million users within two months of its launch. But ChatGPT is just the tip of the generative AI iceberg.


Generative AI's history actually goes back nine decades. Though innovations and developments abound, its progress commercially has been relatively slow -- until recently. The combination of larger labeled data sets, faster computers and new ways of automatically encoding unlabeled data has hastened generative AI's development over the past five years. The last year alone has seen near-sentient chatbots, dozens of new services for generating images from descriptions, and the adaptation of large language models (LLMs) to virtually every aspect of business.

Much of today's developments were built on advancements in computational linguistics and natural language processing. Likewise, early work on procedural content generation has led to content generation in games, and parametric design work has set the stage for industrial design.

90 years and counting
Many key milestones dot the landscape of generative AI's development and innovation.

1932
Georges Artsrouni invented a machine he reportedly called the mechanical brain to translate between languages on a mechanical computer encoded onto punch cards. He built a second version that won the main prize for mechanography at an exhibition in Paris in 1937.

THIS ARTICLE IS PART OF

1957
Linguist Noam Chomsky published Syntactic Structures, which describes grammatical rules for parsing and generating natural language sentences. The book also supports techniques like syntactical parsing and grammar checking.

1963
Computer science professor Ivan Sutherland introduced Sketchpad, an interactive 3D software platform that allowed users to procedurally modify 2D and 3D content. In 1968, Sutherland and fellow professor David Evans started Evans & Sutherland. Some of their students went on to start Pixar, Adobe and Silicon Graphics.

1964
Mathematician and architect Christopher Alexander published Notes on the Synthesis of Form, which spelled out principles for automating design that later influenced the parametric and generative design of products. In 1976, he authored A Pattern Language, which was influential in architecture and inspired new software development approaches.

Chatbot history goes back more than six decades.Chatbots may have evolved slowly for six decades, but in just a matter of months, the launch of ChatGPT has catapulted generative AI into the social consciousness.
1966
MIT professor Joseph Weizenbaum created the first chatbot, Eliza, which simulates conversations with a psychotherapist. MIT News reported in 2008 that Weizenbaum "grew skeptical of AI" and "was shocked to discover that many users were taking his program seriously and were opening their hearts to it."

The Automatic Language Processing Advisory Committee (ALPAC) reported that machine translation and computational linguistics were not living up to their promises and led to research funding cuts in both technologies for the next 20 years.

Mathematician Leonard E. Baum introduced probabilistic hidden Markov models, which were later used in speech recognition, analyzing proteins and generating responses.

1968
Computer science professor Terry Winograd created SHRDLU, the first multimodal AI that could manipulate and reason out a world of blocks according to instructions from a user.

1969
William A. Woods introduced the augmented transition network, a type of graph theoretic structure for translating information into a form that computers could process. He built one of the first natural language systems called LUNAR to answer questions about the Apollo 11 moon rocks for the NASA Manned Spacecraft Center.

1970
Yale computer science and psychology professor Roger Schank, co-founder of the Cognitive Sciences Society, developed the conceptual dependency theory to mathematically describe the processes involved in natural language understanding and reasoning.ChatGPTThe article by George Lawton, published on 10 May 2023, discusses the history and evolution of generative AI, with ChatGPT as a recent example that garnered significant attention and reached over 100 million users within two months. Generative AI has been in the making for nine decades. Its commercial progress was relatively slow until the recent surge in advancements due to larger data sets, faster computing, and new encoding methods for unlabeled data. The past year witnessed breakthroughs like near-sentient chatbots, image generation from text, and the application of large language models (LLMs) in various business areas.Historical milestones include:1932: Georges Artsrouni developed a mechanical machine for language translation.1957: Noam Chomsky's "Syntactic Structures" discussed grammatical rules for natural language.1963: Ivan Sutherland's Sketchpad, an early interactive 3D software, was introduced.1964: Christopher Alexander's work on automating design influenced later generative design approaches.1966: Joseph Weizenbaum's chatbot, Eliza, simulated a therapist's conversation.1968: Terry Winograd's SHRDLU, a pioneering multimodal AI, was developed.1969: William A. Woods' LUNAR system answered questions about Apollo 11 moon rocks.1970: Roger Schank introduced the conceptual dependency theory for natural language understanding.Generative AI's journey spans almost a century, with its current innovations built upon foundational research in computational linguistics and natural language processing.ððªð°ð¯ðªð¤ ðð°ð®ð±ð¶ðµð¦ð³ð´

Self-organizing Systems

Scientists working with bionic computers are hopeful that these strange thinking machines may help break the programming bottleneck. 

Each step a present-day computer is to take must be outlined in detail before the machine can function. It frequently takes months of human time to prepare. 

The mouse, equipped with red and green sensors, won't be instructed - programmed, in computer language - to pay attention to the lights. But, like a live mouse, it will see them.

If predictions hold, the mouse, without being told, will figure out for itself that red means left and green means right. Then, when placed in a new maze equipped with lights, it should run it properly the first time.

Bright Bionic Future

A bionic brain might eventually begin to perform feats now completely out of the question for non-living systems and might display true intelligence.

Pattern recognition, for example, is a field now in its infancy. A few machines have been built which can read several handwritten numbers or letters if they're written clearly enough.

But human beings recognize with ease patterns of astonishing complexity. A child who can't add 2 + 2, for example, can recognize hundreds of different people at a glance by differentiating between the subtle changes in the patterns of their faces.

Elementary self-organizing pattern-recognizing systems called "Perceptrons" have already been built at Cornell and other places. They work but do well to recognize simple letters. 

However, what scientists learn from these early machines may help them build more versatile devices in the future.

A highly sophisticated pattern recognizer would certainly program a computer to solve a problem which it can complete in a few minutes.

Man, animals, and now bionic computers, such as the one which guides the mouse, fall into a category labeled "self-organizing data-processing systems." One man can tell another to make out a payroll, and no further instructions are necessary.

Man, in other words, is self-programming. He operates on general instructions, filling in details for himself. But a computer has to be instructed on each minute step: read certain names and amounts from memory, compute certain figures, etc.

Bionic computers are, to some extent, already self-programming. The mouse doesn't have to be programmed to run the maze - it figures out the process for itself. Furthermore, if the maze is changed, it sets to work and learns the new maze again without being told.

A new mouse, one with 40 neurons, is now being built and should be capable of even more complex behavior. In one experiment, Air Force scientists plan to put red and green lights on either side of the passageway. A green light will shine where a right turn should be made; red will indicate a left.

Bionic Applications

In a satellite, it could look at clouds, spot important patterns and notify the ground station, ignoring everything else. A spy in the sky could look out for missile launchings, troop movements, armament buildups, or other tell-tale signs.

It could look at microphotographs of blood samples and tissue sections and make diagnostic decisions. MIT already has a working model of one such device which can recognize a certain type of cancer cell by looking at it through a microscope.

Bionic brains wouldn't have abilities such as these the moment they rolled off the assembly line. Unlike ordinary computers which are ready to work as soon as the last soldered connection cools, bionic computers would have to learn their jobs.

One Air Force scientist estimates that a newly completed brain might sit on the shelf for a year or more, soaking up information poured into it 24 hours a day. Its "education" received in this way would be comparable in some respects to the education of a human being.

Historical Context

In the early 1950s, the need for computer systems orders of magnitude better than those then in existence became obvious. Scientists began to study the brain, the oldest data handling system in existence, to unlock its secrets.

In 1960, the Air Force called a conference, coined the term "bionics" (from the Greek word "bion," meaning "unit of life"). To everyone's surprise, over 700 scientists attended, presenting dozens of papers on their work.

The Air Force has shown significant interest in this field, envisioning applications such as bionic pilots. The need arises as airplanes become too fast and complicated for human pilots to manage.

Expert Opinions

Opinions on bionic computing's future vary. L. D. Harmon of Bell Labs and Dr. Arthur Samuel of IBM are somewhat skeptical about completely imitating the brain. However, others, like Gordon Pask, are optimistic, envisioning human-like capabilities in bionic brains.

Despite limited bionic computing hardware, many believe in its potential. Former RCA President John H. Burns stated that bionics "holds more promise for human benefit than any temporal force at work in our civilization today."

Accompanying Illustration

This small artificial neuron was developed by Ford Motor Co.'s Aeronutronic Div. as part of its self-organizing machine research work. 

The MIND (Magnetic Integrator Neuron Duplicator) device duplicates in some ways the functions of live nerve cells.

While it cannot imitate a living neuron as closely as can the circuit shown in Fig. 3, it is able to remember and learn from experience when taught by human or mechanical teacher.

Mighty mouse
In 1950, Claude Shannon cobbled together telephone circuits to build a machine that could learn.
By Daniel Kleinarchive page
December 19, 2018

MIT MUSEUM
In a grainy film shot at Bell Laboratories in 1952, mathematician and Bell Labs researcher Claude Shannon, SM â40, PhD â40, stands beside a machine of his own construction. First built in 1950, it was one of the worldâs first examples of machine learning: a robotic maze-solving mouse known as Theseus. The Theseus of ancient Greek mythology navigated a minotaurâs labyrinth and escaped by following a thread heâd used to mark his path. But Shannonâs electromechanical toy was able to ârememberâ its path with the help of telephone relay switches.

As the film begins, Shannonâs wheeled mouse methodically explores its surroundingsâa 25-square maze. Shannon tells viewers that the mazeâs metal walls can be freely rearranged, so Theseus must use a small computing machine to learn the layout anew each time. But the mouse, a tiny wooden device containing a bar magnet and adorned with wire whiskers, is âfar too smallâ to contain a computing machine, he says. Instead, the machinery is hidden beneath the floor of the maze, a series of telephone relay circuits he has repurposed to do something that they had never done before: learn.

Advertisement

Under the metal floor of the maze, an electromagnet mounted on a motor-Â­powered carriage can move north, south, east, and west; as it moves, so does Theseus. Each time its copper whiskers touch one of the metal walls and complete the electric circuit, two things happen. First, the corresponding relay circuitâs switch flips from âonâ to âoff,â recording that space as having a wall on that side. Then Theseus rotates 90Â° clockwise and moves forward. In this way, it systematically moves through the maze until it reaches the target, recording the exits and walls for each square it passes through.

Photo of mouse's path in maze
Photo of mouse's path Theseus maze
These photos, published in Life magazine in 1952, show the path Theseus took while learning a maze pattern and the direct path taken on its second trip through the same maze.

While Theseus works through the maze, the film cuts to other footage as Shannon talks about AT&Tâs telephone network. A minute or so later, the viewer returns to the maze, where Shannon, holding the mouse in his hand, tells us that it has finished learning. Shannon places it back in the maze, andâonce itâs releasedâTheseus and its embedded magnet are guided by the electromagnetic carriage along the pathway indicated by the relay switches left in the âonâ position. In just 18 seconds, it scoots unerringly to the target, a brass piece of cheese that triggers a buzzer when touched by the copper whiskers.

Before working at Bell Labs, Shannon had already made his mark as a student at MIT, where he proved that Boolean logic could be applied to electronic circuits, a concept that would lay the groundwork for digital computing. The eminent cognitive scientist Howard Gardner would later call his 1940 masterâs thesis, âA Symbolic Analysis of Relay and Switching Circuits,â âpossibly the most important, and also the most famous, masterâs thesis of the century.â

In 1948, Shannon had introduced the concept of information theory in âA Mathematical Theory of Communication,â a paper providing mathematical proof that all communication can be expressed digitally. The idea that information could be reduced to 1s and 0s was revolutionary in the 1940s, when large parts of the telephone system were still reliant on operators using manual switchboards to make analog, wired connections between phones. It was so revolutionary, in fact, that âthere were a lot of people who did not agree with Shannon,â says Mazin Gilbert, vice president of advanced technology and systems at AT&T Labs. âThey didnât see the profound breakthrough that he had.â For decades, many doubted that communication could be all digital, and it took years before people started testing and understanding Shannonâs vision. âEven in the â90s, scholars had a hard time understanding what Shannon was talking about,â says Gilbert. âHe was multiple generations ahead of his time.â

I remember in the movie DARYL the robot's brain was housed in a mainframe not in the robot which was more like a terminalChatGPTIndeed, the idea of separating the "brain" or the processing unit from the body or the agent is not new, and it has been a recurring theme in science fiction and early robotics. In Shannon's Theseus project, the maze itself housed the "brain" (the relay circuits) that processed and remembered the pathways, while the mouse served as the mobile agent that interacted with the environment. This setup highlights an important concept: intelligence and cognition can be distributed, and the agent you observe might merely be an interface or a terminal.In the movie "D.A.R.Y.L." (Data Analyzing Robot Youth Lifeform), Daryl is a robot with a human appearance, but his "brain" or processing capabilities are in a remote mainframe. The idea was that Daryl was more like a terminal or a node connected to a more massive, centralized brain. This concept is echoed in modern cloud computing, where devices (like smartphones) access massive computational power and storage from remote servers.This separation also introduces the idea that intelligence or consciousness doesn't necessarily need to be housed within the agent that displays the behavior. It delves into deeper philosophical questions about the nature of consciousness and where "thinking" or "processing" truly resides.Can we add Theseus and Ford Motor Company's MIND to the list?ChatGPTCertainly! If we were to compile a list of pioneering contributions to early machine learning and bionic computing, it might look something like this:Claude Shannon's Theseus (1952):Developed by Claude Shannon at Bell Laboratories.A robotic mouse that could navigate a maze and remember its path using telephone relay switches.The maze itself functioned as the "brain" by recording the pathways, and the mouse was essentially an agent interacting with its environment.Ford Motor Company's MIND (Magnetic Integrator Neuron Duplicator):Developed by Ford Motor Co.'s Aeronutronic Division.An artificial neuron that, in some ways, mimicked the functions of live nerve cells.Capable of learning and remembering when taught by either human or mechanical means.Perceptrons:Early self-organizing pattern-recognizing systems developed at institutions like Cornell.Recognized simple patterns, mainly letters.Bionic Mouse (as described in the "Bionic Computers" article):Equipped with red and green sensors.Capable of self-organizing and learning the significance of light colors in navigating a maze without being explicitly programmed.Other examples from history could be added, depending on the scope and focus of the list. The aforementioned entries, however, capture some of the early and significant efforts in bionic computing and machine learning.1978
Don Worth created the rogue-like game Beneath Apple Manor for the Apple II while a programmer at UCLA. The game used procedural content generation to programmatically create a rich game world that could run on the limited computer hardware available at the time.

1980
Michael Toy and Glenn Wichman developed the Unix-based game Rogue, which used procedural content generation to dynamically generate new game levels. Toy co-founded the company A.I. Design to port the game to the PC a few years later. The game inspired subsequent interest in using procedural content generation in the gaming industry to generate levels, characters, textures and other elements.

1985
Computer scientist and philosopher Judea Pearl introduced Bayesian network causal analysis, which provided statistical techniques for representing uncertainty that led to methods for generating content in a specific style, tone or length.

1986
Michael Irwin Jordan laid the foundation for the modern use of recurrent neural networks (RNNs) with the publication of "Serial order: a parallel distributed processing approach." His innovation used backpropagation to reduce error, which opened the door for further research and the widespread adoption of RNNs for processing language a few years later.

1988
Software provider PTC launched Pro/Engineer, the first application that allowed designers to quickly generate new designs by adjusting parameters and constraints in a controlled fashion. The solid modeling software, now called PTC Creo, helps companies like Caterpillar and John Deere develop industrial equipment faster.

1989
Yann LeCun, Yoshua Bengio and Patrick Haffner demonstrated how convolutional neural networks (CNNs) can be used to recognize images. LeNet-5 was an early implementation of the new techniques for accurately identifying handwritten numbers. Even though it took some time, improvements in computer hardware and labeled data sets made it possible for the new approach to scale up, thanks to the ImageNet database in 2006 and the AlexNet CNN architecture in 2012.ChatGPTThe information you've shared provides an intriguing snapshot of some pivotal moments in the development of computational technologies, machine learning, and content generation from the late 1970s to the late 1980s. Judea Pearl's introduction of Bayesian network causal analysis provides the theoretical foundation for many modern AI systems, especially those dealing with uncertain or incomplete information. Similarly, the advances in recurrent neural networks (RNNs) by Michael Irwin Jordan led to the rise of deep learning models that are so popular today, especially for tasks like natural language processing.The introduction of convolutional neural networks (CNNs) by Yann LeCun and his colleagues in the late 1980s represents another seminal moment in the history of AI. This kind of software streamlines the design process and demonstrates how computational techniques have implications beyond pure data processing â they affect tangible, physical products as well.Taken together, these developments sketch out a trajectory of increasing sophistication and capability in computer systems and AI, setting the stage for the modern era of machine learning and data science.I think we should take a moment to examine Pearl's reasoning, particularly with respect to Markov blankets and substrate independence of DAG's and Do-Calculus.ChatGPTCertainly, Judea Pearl's contributions to artificial intelligence and statistics are profound. Let's delve into some of these concepts:Bayesian Networks & DAGs (Directed Acyclic Graphs):
Pearl introduced Bayesian networks as a representation for encoding probabilistic relationships among a set of variables. A Bayesian network is a DAG where nodes represent variables, and edges indicate a direct causal influence from one variable to another. Conditional probabilities are associated with each node, providing a compact representation for the joint probability distribution over all variables.Markov Blanket:
For a given node in a Bayesian network, its Markov blanket consists of its parents, its children, and its children's other parents. Intuitively, the Markov blanket of a node contains all the variables that shield the node from the rest of the network. This means that if you know the values of the nodes in the Markov blanket, then the rest of the network becomes irrelevant for predicting the value of the node.Do-Calculus:
One of Pearl's most significant contributions is the introduction of the do-calculus, which provides a formal system for reasoning about interventions in causal models. While traditional Bayesian networks allow us to make inferences about correlations and probabilistic dependencies, the do-calculus lets us make predictions about the effects of interventions (e.g., what would happen if we set a particular variable to a certain value). This is critical for answering causal questions and making decisions based on models.Substrate Independence:
Pearl's formalism for reasoning about causality is substrate-independent, meaning it's not tied to any specific domain or substrate. This universality makes his framework incredibly powerful. Whether you're studying biology, economics, or any other domain, the same principles apply when trying to tease out causal relationships from data.Judea Pearl's work bridges the gap between mere statistical associations and true causal relationships, providing a rigorous framework for thinking about how variables influence each other. His introduction of tools like the do-calculus has had profound implications for fields ranging from epidemiology to economics, allowing researchers to make causal inferences from observational data.A summary of the conversation so far.2 / 2The previous model used in this conversation is unavailable. We've switched you to the latest default model.ChatGPTIn our conversation, we've explored various facets of artificial intelligence, machine learning, and notable historical developments in the field. Sepp Hochreiter and Jurgen Schmidhuber introduced the Long Short-Term Memory (LSTM) architecture, which helped overcome some of the problems with RNNs. LSTM gave RNNs support for memory and helped spur research into tools for analyzing longer text sequences.

A team of researchers at Bell Communications Research, the University of Chicago and the University of Western Ontario published the paper "Indexing by Latent Semantic Analysis." The new technique provided a method to identify the semantic relationship between words found in a sample of training text, paving the way for deep learning techniques like word2vec and BERT (Bidirectional Encoder Representations from Transformers).ChatGPTThe year 1990 marked significant developments in the field of artificial intelligence and deep learning:Long Short-Term Memory (LSTM):Sepp Hochreiter and Jurgen Schmidhuber introduced the LSTM architecture.LSTM addressed issues with traditional Recurrent Neural Networks (RNNs) by introducing the concept of memory cells.LSTM's ability to capture and utilize long-range dependencies in sequences made it a breakthrough in the field and contributed to the analysis of longer text sequences."Indexing by Latent Semantic Analysis":A team of researchers from Bell Communications Research, the University of Chicago, and the University of Western Ontario published this paper.The technique introduced in the paper allowed for the identification of semantic relationships between words based on a sample of training text.This development laid the foundation for subsequent deep learning techniques like word2vec and BERT (Bidirectional Encoder Representations from Transformers), which have played pivotal roles in natural language understanding and processing.These milestones in 1990 furthered the progress of deep learning and natural language processing, contributing to the development of more advanced models and tools for analyzing text and sequences.2000
Yoshua Bengio, Rejean Ducharme, Pascal Vincent and Christian Jauvin at the University of Montreal published "A Neural Probabilistic Language Model," which suggested a method to model language using feed-forward neural networks. The paper led to further research into techniques to encode words into vectors representing their meaning and context automatically. It also demonstrated how backpropagation could help train RNNs for modeling languages.ChatGPTIn the year 2000, a noteworthy development in the field of natural language processing and neural networks occurred:"A Neural Probabilistic Language Model" (University of Montreal):Published by Yoshua Bengio, Rejean Ducharme, Pascal Vincent, and Christian Jauvin at the University of Montreal.The paper introduced a novel approach to modeling language using feed-forward neural networks.It proposed a method for encoding words into vectors that represent their meaning and context automatically, marking the early steps toward word embeddings.Importantly, the paper also demonstrated how backpropagation could effectively train Recurrent Neural Networks (RNNs) for language modeling, highlighting the importance of neural networks in natural language processing tasks.This research laid the foundation for subsequent breakthroughs in language representation, word embeddings, and the use of neural networks for various language-related tasks, revolutionizing the field of natural language understanding and processing.2006
Data scientist Fei-Fei Li set up the ImageNet database, which laid the foundation for visual object recognition. The database planted the seeds for advances in recognizing objects with AlexNet and generating them later.

IBM Watson originated with the initial goal of beating a human on the iconic quiz show Jeopardy!. In 2011, the question-answering computer system defeated the show's all-time (human) champion Ken Jennings.

2011
Apple released Siri, a voice-powered personal assistant that can generate responses and take actions in response to voice requests.ChatGPTThe year 2006 and 2011 brought significant advancements in artificial intelligence and natural language processing:2006:ImageNet Database by Fei-Fei Li:Data scientist Fei-Fei Li established the ImageNet database, a pivotal development in the field of computer vision.ImageNet provided a vast collection of labeled images, serving as a benchmark for training and evaluating visual object recognition algorithms.This database laid the foundation for significant progress in object recognition, including breakthroughs achieved with models like AlexNet, which later played a crucial role in the development of deep learning techniques for image classification.2011:IBM Watson's Triumph on Jeopardy!:IBM's Watson, initially created with the goal of beating human contestants on the quiz show Jeopardy!, achieved a remarkable milestone in 2011.Watson competed against and defeated Ken Jennings, one of the show's all-time human champions.This event showcased the potential of AI systems for natural language understanding and question-answering, marking a significant achievement in cognitive computing.Release of Apple's Siri:In 2011, Apple introduced Siri, a voice-powered personal assistant, as part of its iOS ecosystem.Siri could generate responses and take actions in response to voice commands, making it one of the early examples of conversational AI and voice-activated assistants.Siri's release marked a significant step in the integration of AI-driven voice interaction into consumer devices and services, paving the way for the broader adoption of virtual assistants in various applications.These milestones highlight the rapid progression of AI technologies in understanding and processing both visual and textual information, as well as their integration into everyday consumer devices and services.2012
Alex Krizhevsky designed the AlexNet CNN architecture, pioneering a new way of automatically training neural networks that takes advantage of recent GPU advances. In the ImageNet Large Scale Visual Recognition Challenge that year, AlexNet recognized images with an error rate more than 10.8% lower than the runner-up. It inspired research into scaling deep learning algorithms in parallel on GPUs.ChatGPTThe year 2012 was a landmark in the advancement of deep learning and computer vision, primarily due to the introduction of AlexNet:AlexNet (Alex Krizhevsky):Alex Krizhevsky designed the AlexNet Convolutional Neural Network (CNN) architecture.This architecture marked a significant breakthrough in computer vision and deep learning.It effectively leveraged recent advances in Graphics Processing Units (GPUs) to train neural networks more efficiently and at scale.In the ImageNet Large Scale Visual Recognition Challenge of 2012, AlexNet demonstrated its capabilities by recognizing images with an error rate more than 10.8% lower than the runner-up.The success of AlexNet inspired further research into deep learning algorithms and their parallelization on GPUs, driving rapid progress in the field of computer vision and image classification.AlexNet's achievements not only demonstrated the power of deep neural networks for image recognition but also catalyzed the adoption of GPUs and distributed computing in the training of large-scale neural networks, laying the foundation for many subsequent breakthroughs in AI and machine learning.2013
Google researcher Tomas Mikolov and colleagues introduced word2vec to identify semantic relationships between words automatically. This technique made it easier to transform raw text into vectors that deep learning algorithms could process.

2014
Research scientist Ian Goodfellow developed generative adversarial networks, which pit two neural networks against each other to generate increasingly realistic content. One neural network generates new content while the other discriminates between real and generated data. Improvements in both networks led to better quality content over time.

Diederik Kingma and Max Welling introduced variational autoencoders for generative modeling. VAEs are used to generate images, videos and text. The algorithm found better ways of representing input data and transforming back to the original or into another format.ChatGPTThe years 2013 and 2014 brought forth significant advancements in natural language processing and generative modeling:2013:word2vec (Tomas Mikolov):Google researcher Tomas Mikolov and his colleagues introduced word2vec, a groundbreaking technique for identifying semantic relationships between words automatically.Word2vec made it easier to transform raw text into vectors, enabling deep learning algorithms to process and understand textual data more effectively.This approach greatly contributed to the development of word embeddings, which represent words as dense vectors in a continuous space, allowing AI models to capture and leverage word semantics and context.2014:Generative Adversarial Networks (GANs) (Ian Goodfellow):Research scientist Ian Goodfellow pioneered the concept of Generative Adversarial Networks (GANs).GANs involve two neural networks, a generator and a discriminator, competing against each other.The generator creates new content, such as images or text, while the discriminator evaluates the content for realism.Over time, improvements in both networks lead to the generation of increasingly realistic and high-quality content.GANs have since become a cornerstone in the field of generative modeling and have found applications in image generation, text generation, and more.Variational Autoencoders (VAEs) (Diederik Kingma and Max Welling):Diederik Kingma and Max Welling introduced Variational Autoencoders (VAEs), a technique for generative modeling.VAEs are used to generate various types of data, including images, videos, and text.They provide a more structured way of representing input data and transforming it back to its original or different formats.VAEs have been influential in tasks like image generation, data compression, and representation learning.These breakthroughs in 2013 and 2014 greatly expanded the capabilities of AI systems in understanding and generating natural language and content. They opened up new avenues for applications in text analysis, image generation, and generative modeling, which continue to be actively researched and applied today.2015
Autodesk began publishing research on Project Dreamcatcher, a generative design tool that uses algorithms to create new designs. Users can describe intended properties such as materials, size and weight.

Stanford researchers published work on diffusion models in the paper "Deep Unsupervised Learning using Nonequilibrium Thermodynamics." The technique provided a way to reverse engineer the process of adding noise to a final image. It synthesizes pictures and videos, generates text and models languages.

2016
Microsoft released the chatbot TAY (thinking about you), which responded to questions submitted via Twitter. Users soon began tweeting inflammatory concepts to the chatbot, which quickly generated racist and sexually charged messages in response. Microsoft shut it down after 16 hours.

2017
Google announced work on using AI to design the TPU (Tensor Processing Unit) chip for deep learning workloads.

Google researchers developed the concept of transformers in the seminal paper "Attention is all you need." The article inspired subsequent research into tools that could automatically parse unlabeled text into LLMs.

Siemens partnered with Frustum to integrate generative design capabilities into the Siemens NX product design tools. The new design variations. PTC, a Siemens competitor, acquired Frustum the following year for its own generative design offering.

Autodesk debuted a commercial implementation of its Project Dreamcatcher research as Autodesk Generative Design.

Google researchers implemented transformers into BERT, which was trained on more than 3.3 billion words, consisted of 110 million parameters and could automatically learn the relationship between words in sentences, paragraphs and even books to predict the meaning of text.

Google DeepMind researchers developed AlphaFold for predicting protein structures. The innovative technology laid the foundation for generative AI applications in medical research, drug development and chemistry.

OpenAI released GPT (Generative Pre-trained Transformer). Trained on about 40 gigabytes of data and consisting of 117 million parameters, GPT paved the way for subsequent LLMs in content generation, chatbots and language translation.

2019
The Malaria No More charity and soccer star David Beckham used deep fake technology to translate his speech and facial movements into nine languages as part of an urgent appeal to end malaria worldwide.

A U.K.-based energy firm CEO transferred â¬220,000 ($243,000) to a Hungarian bank after hackers impersonated his parent company boss using an audio deep fake with an urgent request. And the world was alerted to a new era of social engineering cyber attacks.

OpenAI released GPT-2 with 1.5 billion parameters. Trained on a data set of eight million webpages, GPT-2's objective was to predict the next word, given all the previous words within some text.

Open AI released GPT-3, the largest ever neural network consisting of 175 billion parameters and requiring 800 gigabytes to store. In the first nine months, OpenAI reported that more than 300 applications were using GPT-3 and thousands of developers were building on the platform.

Researchers at Google, the University of California, Berkeley and UC San Diego published "NeRF: Representing Scenes as Neural Radiance Fields for View Synthesis." The new technique galvanized research and innovation into 3D content generation.

Microsoft researchers developed VIsual VOcabulary (VIVO) pretraining of image captioning algorithms without captioned data. Tests indicated this training can surpass many human captioners.
ChatGPTThe year 2020 marked significant milestones in the field of AI and deep learning:GPT-3 (OpenAI):OpenAI released GPT-3, a groundbreaking neural network, which became the largest-ever language model with an astonishing 175 billion parameters.GPT-3's vast size required 800 gigabytes to store, making it one of the most powerful AI models to date.In just the first nine months after its release, more than 300 applications were reported to be using GPT-3, and thousands of developers were actively building upon this platform.GPT-3's capabilities in natural language understanding and generation had a profound impact on various applications, from chatbots to content generation.NeRF (Neural Radiance Fields):Researchers at Google, the University of California, Berkeley, and UC San Diego published the paper "NeRF: Representing Scenes as Neural Radiance Fields for View Synthesis."This innovative technique sparked a wave of research and innovation in the field of 3D content generation, offering new possibilities for creating realistic and immersive virtual environments.VIVO (Visual Vocabulary):Microsoft researchers developed VIsual VOcabulary (VIVO), a pretraining method for image captioning algorithms that did not rely on captioned data.Tests showed that VIVO pretraining could surpass the performance of many human captioners, demonstrating the potential for AI to excel in tasks related to image understanding and description.These developments highlighted the increasing scale and capabilities of neural networks, their application across diverse domains, and their potential to outperform human performance in certain tasks, leading to exciting advancements in AI and deep learning.2021
Cerebras Systems used AI to help generate the design for the WSE-2, a single chip the size of a complete silicon wafer with more than 850,000 cores and 2.6 trillion transistors.

OpenAI introduced Dall-E, which can generate images from text prompts. The name is a combination of WALL-E, the name of a fictional robot, and the artist Salvador Dali. The new tool introduced the concept of contrastive language-image pretraining (CLIP) to rank captions for images found on the internet.

2022
OpenAI released Dall-E 2, a smaller and more efficient image generator that uses a diffusion model to generate images. The AI system can generate images and art from a description in natural language.

Nvidia created NGP Instant NeRF code for quickly transforming pictures into 3D images and content.

Google DeepMind released a paper on Gato, a general-purpose multimodal AI that can perform more than 600 tasks, including captioning text, generating robot instructions, playing video games and navigating environments.

Researchers from Runway Research, Stability AI and CompVis LMU released Stable Diffusion as open source code that can automatically generate image content from a text prompt. This technique is a new method of combining an auto-encoder to transform data into an intermediate format so Diffusion models can process it more efficiently.

OpenAI released ChatGPT in November to provide a chat-based interface to its GPT 3.5 LLM. It attracted more than 100 million users within two months, representing the fastest ever consumer adoption of a service.

2023
Getty Images and a group of artists separately sued several companies that implemented Stable Diffusion for copyright infringement. Their suit claimed Stability AI, Midjourney and DeviantArt, among others, scraped Getty's content without consent.

Microsoft integrated a version of ChatGPT into its Bing search engine. Google quickly followed with plans to release the Bard chat service based on its Lamda engine. And the controversy over detecting AI- generated content heated up.

OpenAI released GPT-4 multimodal LLM, which can receive both text and image prompts. A who's who of technology leaders, including Elon Musk, Steve Wozniak and thousands more signatories, called for a pause on the development of advanced AI systems "more powerful than GPT-4."ChatGPTThe technology adoption.Generative AI's future
The depth and ease of using ChatGPT have shown great promise for the widespread adoption of generative AI. But problems in rolling out the chatbot safely and responsibly have inspired research into better tools for detecting AI-generated text, images and video. Industry and society will also build better tools for tracking the provenance of information used to create more trustworthy AI.

Improvements in AI development platforms will help accelerate research and development of better generative AI capabilities for text, images, video, 3D content, pharmaceuticals, supply chains, logistics and business processes. As good as these new one-off tools are, the most significant impact of generative AI will be realized when these capabilities are embedded directly into better versions of today's tools.

Grammar checkers will improve. Design tools will seamlessly embed more useful recommendations directly into workflows. And training tools will automatically identify best practices in one area of an organization to help train others more efficiently.ChatGPTThe future of generative AI holds great promise and will likely see advancements in various areas:Safety and Responsibility:Researchers are actively working on better tools for detecting AI-generated text, images, and videos to ensure safe and responsible use of generative AI.Building tools for tracking the origin of information will enhance trustworthiness in AI-generated content.Diverse Applications:Generative AI will continue to advance in various domains, including text, images, video, 3D content, pharmaceuticals, supply chains, logistics, and business processes.Integration:The integration of generative AI capabilities into existing tools and workflows will be a significant driver of its impact.Grammar checkers, design tools, and training tools will seamlessly incorporate generative AI to provide more useful recommendations and improve efficiency in various sectors.Generative AI is poised to transform the way we create content, make decisions, and optimize processes, ultimately leading to more efficient and innovative applications across industries.A list of all of the topics we've discussed.ChatGPTCertainly, here is a list of the topics we've discussed during our conversation:History of Generative AI Innovations (spanning nine decades)Theseus - Using the annealed state as a bitmap for its own source code is a concept where you represent the final state of an Ising model, typically after a simulation has annealed (reached a stable state), as a visual pattern similar to a QR code. Here's a step-by-step explanation:Annealing: In the context of Ising models, annealing refers to the process of gradually cooling the system from a high-temperature, disordered state to a low-temperature, ordered state. It's a way to represent images or patterns.Source Code: This refers to the actual code or program that describes the Ising model simulation. It's a complex but powerful method for studying the behavior of dark matter and voids in the universe.A refresher on voronoi tesselation and delauney triangulationsChatGPTCertainly! Voronoi tessellation and Delaunay triangulation are fundamental concepts in computational geometry, and they are closely related. Let's refresh our understanding of both:Voronoi Tessellation:Definition: Voronoi tessellation, also known as Voronoi diagram or Voronoi partition, is a way to divide a plane into regions based on the distance to a given set of points. Each region in the tessellation is associated with one of these points and consists of all the points on the plane that are closer to that particular point than to any other point in the set.Key Features:Voronoi regions are polygons (usually convex).The boundaries of Voronoi regions are equidistant from the two nearest points in the given set.Voronoi tessellations have applications in various fields, such as computational geometry, geography, and modeling.Construction:To construct a Voronoi tessellation, you start with a set of points (called generators or sites) on the plane.For each generator, you find the region of points that are closer to it than to any other generator. These regions collectively form the Voronoi diagram.Use Cases:In geography, Voronoi tessellations can be used to divide a map into regions based on the nearest post offices, stores, or other facilities.In computer graphics, they are used for various tasks like texture mapping and generating procedural patterns.Delaunay Triangulation:Definition: Delaunay triangulation is a way to connect a set of points on a plane with non-overlapping triangles so that no point falls inside the circumcircle (the circle passing through all three vertices) of any triangle.Key Features:Delaunay triangulations have several desirable properties, such as maximizing the minimum angle of all triangles (avoiding skinny triangles).They are unique for a given set of points.Triangles in a Delaunay triangulation tend to be relatively uniform in shape.Construction:To construct a Delaunay triangulation, you start with a set of points (sites).Connect the points to form triangles in a way that satisfies the Delaunay condition (no point is inside the circumcircle of any triangle).This can be done using various algorithms, such as the incremental Delaunay triangulation.Use Cases:Delaunay triangulations are commonly used in computer graphics for mesh generation, terrain modeling, and finite element analysis.They are also used in geographic information systems (GIS) for interpolating data and creating TINs (triangulated irregular networks).Relationship:There is a close relationship between Voronoi tessellation and Delaunay triangulation. Given a set of points, the Voronoi diagram and the Delaunay triangulation are dual structures. This means that the edges of the Delaunay triangulation are the same as the edges of the Voronoi diagram, and vice versa.This duality property has practical implications. For example, if you have one structure (e.g., the Delaunay triangulation), you can easily derive the other (the Voronoi diagram) and vice versa. Algorithms for constructing one often leverage the other to optimize their performance.In summary, Voronoi tessellation divides a plane into regions based on proximity to a set of points, while Delaunay triangulation connects points with non-overlapping triangles. Understanding these concepts is valuable in various fields, especially in computational geometry and spatial analysis.We were also going to use force based graphs to visualize vector spaces such as word2vec and probability space in Geometric BayesianismChatGPTUsing force-based graphs to visualize vector spaces, including those generated by techniques like Word2Vec and probability spaces in Geometric Bayesianism, can be a powerful approach to gain insights and understand the relationships between data points. Here's an overview of this visualization technique:Force-Based Graphs:Force-based graph drawing algorithms are commonly used to create visual representations of graphs, networks, or vector spaces. These algorithms simulate physical forces between nodes (points) to determine their positions in the visualization. Nodes in the graph are typically connected by edges, and the goal is to arrange the nodes in a way that minimizes energy (i.e., forces) and optimally represents the relationships between nodes.Visualizing Vector Spaces:Word2Vec: Word2Vec is a technique for representing words or phrases as vectors in a continuous vector space. These vectors capture semantic relationships between words. To visualize Word2Vec embeddings using a force-based graph approach:Each word is represented as a node in the graph.Edges between nodes represent semantic similarity or co-occurrence relationships between words.Force-based algorithms are applied to position words in the vector space so that semantically similar words are closer to each other.Geometric Bayesianism: Geometric Bayesianism is a framework for modeling probability distributions over geometric spaces. When visualizing probability spaces in Geometric Bayesianism:Data points or probability distributions are represented as nodes in the graph.Edges between nodes can represent dependencies, correlations, or conditional probabilities.Force-based graph algorithms help position nodes in a way that reflects the underlying probabilistic relationships.Steps for Visualization:Data Preparation: Prepare your data, which may consist of vectors (e.g., Word2Vec embeddings) or probability distributions.Graph Construction: Create a graph representation of your data. Define nodes to represent data points, and establish edges to capture relationships. For Word2Vec, edges may represent semantic similarity, while for Geometric Bayesianism, they may represent probabilistic dependencies.Force-Based Layout: Apply a force-based layout algorithm to arrange the nodes. Common algorithms include:Fruchterman-Reingold: It models attractive forces between connected nodes and repulsive forces between all nodes.ForceAtlas2: It balances forces to optimize the graph's layout.Spring Embedders: These simulate springs between nodes to achieve equilibrium.Visual Encoding: Assign visual attributes to nodes and edges to represent characteristics of the data, such as color-coding nodes based on categories or using edge thickness to indicate strength of relationships.Interactive Exploration: Provide interactivity for users to explore the graph, zoom in/out, and access additional information about nodes and edges.Analysis: Use the visualization to analyze patterns, clusters, or anomalies in your data.Tools: Various data visualization libraries and tools support force-based graph layouts, including D3.js, Gephi, NetworkX (for Python), and others.Visualizing vector spaces and probability spaces using force-based graphs can help reveal hidden structures and relationships within your data, making it easier to interpret and derive insights from complex high-dimensional data.I am going to need help understanding Tristan Needham's visual complex analyst and Karl Fant's process invocation using ring bubble pipelines and propogating null wavefronts in null convention logic to represent Markov blanketsChatGPTUnderstanding Tristan Needham's "Visual Complex Analysis" and Karl Fant's concepts related to process invocation using ring bubble pipelines and propagating null wavefronts in null convention logic to represent Markov blankets can be complex, but I'll provide a simplified overview:Visual Complex Analysis (Tristan Needham):Complex Analysis: This branch of mathematics deals with complex numbers, which have both real and imaginary components (e.g., a + bi, where 'a' and 'b' are real numbers, and 'i' is the imaginary unit).Visual Approach: Tristan Needham's work focuses on providing visual and geometric interpretations of complex analysis concepts. He aims to make complex analysis more intuitive and understandable by using diagrams, illustrations, and geometric representations.Complex Functions: Needham explores how complex functions (functions that take complex numbers as inputs and produce complex numbers as outputs) can be visualized in terms of mappings and transformations of geometric shapes.Process Invocation with Ring Bubble Pipelines (Karl Fant):Process Invocation: This concept relates to how different processes or tasks are initiated and executed within a computational system.Ring Bubble Pipelines: This term suggests a computational architecture or framework where data and processes flow through interconnected stages or components arranged in a ring-like structure.Propagation of Null Wavefronts: In computing, a "wavefront" often represents the progress of a computational process or a signal through a system. "Null wavefronts" might refer to situations where no actual computation or significant data propagation occurs at a particular stage, possibly due to certain conditions being met.Null Convention Logic: This is a logic design approach where the system can handle "null" or undefined values in a predictable manner. It's often used in fault-tolerant systems or asynchronous circuits.Markov Blankets:A Markov blanket, in the context of probabilistic graphical models, refers to a set of variables that, when known, completely shields a target variable from the influence of all other variables in the system. It represents a form of conditional independence.Markov blankets play a crucial role in Bayesian networks and graphical models, as they help simplify complex probability distributions and make probabilistic reasoning more tractable.Understanding these concepts in detail would likely require delving into the specific works of Tristan Needham and Karl Fant, as well as the broader mathematical and computational domains they belong to. Let's consolidate the information:

**Visual Complex Analysis (Tristan Needham):**
- The book introduces complex analysis using intuitive geometric visualizations.
- It offers a deep understanding through diagrams, animations, and visual explanations.
- Key visualizations include the Riemann sphere, conformal mappings, and the Cauchy-Riemann equations.
- This approach finds applications in physics, engineering, computer graphics, and image processing.

**Practical Applications for Visualizing Complex Analysis:**
- In **Physics**, visual techniques help in understanding fluid dynamics and complex potential functions.
- **Engineering** benefits from visualizing concepts like complex impedance and frequency responses, especially in electrical engineering.
- **Computer Graphics** use complex analysis visualizations for tasks like texture mapping, 3D modeling, and image effects.
- **Fractals** like the Mandelbrot set offer insights through visual patterns stemming from complex numbers.

**Process Invocation with Ring Bubble Pipelines (Karl Fant):**
- Process invocation controls computational operations in a system.
- Ring bubble pipelines organize processes in a circular structure, ensuring sequential data transfer.
- "Propagating null wavefronts" is a method of transmitting signals, especially when data is not available.
- Null convention logic provides reliability by offering conventions for handling null wavefronts and ensuring consistent behavior.
  
**Handling Errors with Null Convention Logic:**
- Null wavefronts signal the absence of meaningful data.
- The logic includes error detection and consistent behavior in the presence of errors.
- It offers fault tolerance, allowing systems to operate despite errors.

**Markov Blankets:**
- Represents a set of variables that makes a target variable conditionally independent from others.
- It defines the minimum variable set needed for accurate prediction.
- Key applications include Bayesian networks and Markov random fields.

**Techniques for Identifying Markov Blankets:**
- **Constraint-Based Methods** use statistical tests to determine conditional independence.
- **Score-Based Methods** evaluate models' fit using scoring metrics.
- **Learning Algorithms** leverage statistical inference to automatically identify Markov blankets.
- **Expert Knowledge** can provide insights into variable relationships.

In summary, "Visual Complex Analysis" provides a geometric approach to understanding complex analysis, finding applications across multiple domains. Ring bubble pipelines, coupled with null convention logic, offer a structured approach to process invocation and handling errors. Markov blankets, essential in probabilistic graphical models, can be identified using a blend of statistical methods and expert insights.ChatGPTYou've summarized the key points concisely! there any evidence in the effectiveness of progression muscle relaxation for physical or mental health?ChatGPTProgressive Muscle Relaxation (PMR) is a relaxation technique developed by Edmund Jacobson in the 1920s. It involves tensing and then relaxing muscle groups in a systematic manner to reduce physical and mental tension. PMR is widely used and has shown several benefits for both physical and mental health:Physical Health:Stress Reduction: PMR is effective in reducing stress, which can have a positive impact on physical health. Chronic stress is linked to various health issues, including cardiovascular problems, weakened immune function, and digestive disorders. PMR helps counteract the physical effects of stress.Pain Management: PMR can be used as a complementary therapy for managing chronic pain conditions, such as fibromyalgia and tension headaches. By promoting muscle relaxation, it may alleviate muscle tension contributing to pain.Sleep Improvement: People who practice PMR often report improved sleep quality. Better sleep is essential for overall physical health and well-being.Mental Health:Anxiety Reduction: PMR is effective in reducing symptoms of anxiety. It helps individuals become more aware of bodily sensations and teaches them how to relax, which can counter anxiety-related muscle tension.Depression: While not a standalone treatment for depression, PMR can be a useful part of a comprehensive approach. It may help alleviate some physical symptoms associated with depression, such as muscle tension and restlessness.Enhanced Emotional Well-being: Regular PMR practice can lead to increased emotional awareness and a greater sense of relaxation and calm, contributing to overall mental well-being.Coping with Trauma: PMR is sometimes used as a part of trauma-focused therapy to help individuals manage the physical and emotional effects of trauma.It's important to note that the effectiveness of PMR may vary from person to person. Some individuals may find it highly beneficial, while others may not experience the same level of relief. PMR is often used in combination with other relaxation techniques, mindfulness, and therapeutic approaches for maximum effectiveness.As with any health-related practice, it's advisable to consult with a healthcare professional or therapist before starting PMR, especially if you have any underlying medical or mental health conditions. They can provide guidance on its suitability for your specific needs and circumstances.I thought we could use it as an analogyChatGPTCertainly, using Progressive Muscle Relaxation (PMR) as an analogy is a creative way to illustrate certain concepts or processes. Slupska, Julia, War, Health, & Ecosystem: Generative Metaphors in Cybersecurity Governance. Philosophy & Technology, 2020 Forthcoming

30 Pages
Posted: 16 Mar 2020
Julia Slupska
Oxford Internet Institute

Date Written: February 8, 2020

Abstract
Policy-makers involved in cybersecurity governance should pay close attention to the âgenerative metaphorsâ they use to describe and understand new technologies. Generative metaphors structure our understanding of policy problems by imposing mental models of both the problem and possible solutions. As a result, they can also constrain ethical reasoning about new technologies, by uncritically carrying over assumptions about moral roles and obligations from an existing domain. The discussion of global governance of cybersecurity problems has to date been dominated by the metaphor of âcyber warâ. In this paper, I argue that this metaphor diminishes possibilities for international collaboration in this area by limiting states to reactive policies of naming and shaming rather than proactive actions to address systemic features of cyberspace. I suggest that alternative metaphors â such as health, ecosystem, and architecture â can help expose the dominance of the war metaphor and provide a more collaborative and conceptually accurate frame for negotiations.

Keywords: cyber norms, cybersecurity, governance, conceptual metaphors, generative metaphors, cyber warChatGPTThe paper "War, Health, & Ecosystem: Generative Metaphors in Cybersecurity Governance" by Julia Slupska explores the role of generative metaphors in shaping policy and governance in the field of cybersecurity. Generative metaphors are used to structure our understanding of complex issues and can influence ethical reasoning.The central argument in the paper is that the predominant metaphor of "cyber war" used in discussions of cybersecurity governance has limitations. This metaphor tends to lead to reactive policies focused on naming and shaming rather than proactive actions to address the systemic aspects of cyberspace. Slupska suggests that alternative metaphors like "health," "ecosystem," and "architecture" could offer a more collaborative and conceptually accurate framework for addressing cybersecurity challenges.This paper highlights the importance of the language and metaphors used in policy discussions, as they can significantly influence the approaches taken to address complex issues. By considering alternative metaphors, policymakers may be able to promote international collaboration and develop more effective cybersecurity governance strategies.The excerpt you provided is from the book "Metaphor and Thought" edited by Andrew Ortony. It features a chapter by Donald A. Schon titled "Generative metaphor: A perspective on problem-setting in social policy." In this chapter, Schon explores the concept of generative metaphor and its role in shaping our perspectives on the world, influencing how we think about things, make sense of reality, and set the problems we later try to solve.

Schon argues that metaphor is not merely a figure of speech or a linguistic anomaly, but rather a fundamental aspect of our cognition. He introduces the concept of generative metaphor, which refers to both a certain mode of thinking and a process by which new perspectives on the world emerge. Generative metaphors involve the "carrying over" of frames or perspectives from one domain of experience to another, enabling us to see things in new ways.

The chapter addresses two central puzzles related to generative metaphor. The first puzzle is the hermeneutic problem, which concerns the interpretation of texts and understanding how people think about problematic situations through generative metaphors. The second puzzle is the question of generativity itself, exploring how we come to see things in new ways through generative metaphor.

Schon then applies these ideas to the domain of social policy. He argues that the framing of social policy problems is often influenced by the underlying metaphors people use to describe those problems. By examining the generative metaphors that shape problem-setting stories in social policy, we can gain critical insights into how problems are perceived and how potential solutions are framed.

The chapter also discusses the importance of becoming aware of these generative metaphors in order to improve the analysis of social policy problems. Schon suggests that by examining the analogies and disanalogies between the metaphors used and the actual problematic situations, we can enhance the rigor and precision of our analysis.

Additionally, Schon explores the idea of conflicting frames in social policy and suggests that it may be possible to achieve a restructuring and coordination of these frames through inquiry. He draws parallels between the process of frame restructuring and the making of generative metaphor, highlighting their similarities and the potential for mutual illumination in understanding these cognitive processes.

Overall, the chapter explores the role of generative metaphor in shaping our perspectives, problem-setting, and social policy analysis, emphasizing the importance of understanding and critically examining the underlying metaphors that influence our thinking.ChatGPTThank you for providing a detailed summary of Donald A.
